ns-0 ns-subject page-D\xc3\xa1l_Riata rootpage-D\xc3\xa1l_Riata skin-vector action-view">\t\t<div id="mw-page-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="mw-head-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n<div id="mw-indicator-good-star" class="mw-indicator"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Good_articles" title="This is a good article. Follow the link for more information."><img alt="This is a good article. Follow the link for more information." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/19px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png" width="19" height="20" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/29px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Symbol_support_vote.svg/39px-Symbol_support_vote.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="185" /></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Dalriada" redirects here. For the neighbouring Irish kingdom sometimes called Dalaradia, see <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_nAraidi" title="D\xc3\xa1l nAraidi">D\xc3\xa1l nAraidi</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Dalriada_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Dalriada (disambiguation)">Dalriada (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dalriada.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Dalriada.png/200px-Dalriada.png" width="200" height="283" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Dalriada.png/300px-Dalriada.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Dalriada.png/400px-Dalriada.png 2x" data-file-width="794" data-file-height="1123" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dalriada.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite image of Scotland and Ireland showing the approximate greatest extent of D\xc3\xa1l Riata (shaded). The mountainous spine which separates the east and west coasts of Scotland can be seen.</div></div></div>\n<p><b>D\xc3\xa1l Riata</b> or <b>D\xc3\xa1l Riada</b> (also <b>Dalriada</b>) (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/\xc3\xa6/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">\xc3\xa6</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/i\xcb\x90/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">i\xcb\x90</span><span title="/\xc9\x99/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">\xc9\x99</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/\xc9\x99/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">\xc9\x99</span></span>/</a></span></span>) was a <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> <a href="/wiki/Overkingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Overkingdom">overkingdom</a> that included parts of western <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and northeastern <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, on each side of the <a href="/wiki/North_Channel_(Great_Britain_and_Ireland)" title="North Channel (Great Britain and Ireland)">North Channel</a>. At its height in the late 6th and early 7th centuries, it encompassed roughly what is now <a href="/wiki/Argyll" title="Argyll">Argyll</a> in Scotland and part of <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">County Antrim</a> in the Irish province of <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion1_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion1-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Argyll" title="Argyll">Argyll</a>, it consisted of four main <a href="/wiki/Clan" title="Clan">kindreds</a> each with their own chief:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Loarn_mac_Eirc" title="Loarn mac Eirc">Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn</a> (kindred of Loarn) in north and mid-Argyll, who gave their name to the district of <a href="/wiki/Lorne,_Scotland" title="Lorne, Scotland">Lorn</a><sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion1_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion1-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cen%C3%A9l_n%C3%93engusa" title="Cen\xc3\xa9l n\xc3\x93engusa">Cen\xc3\xa9l n\xc3\x93engusa</a> (kindred of \xc3\x93engus) based on <a href="/wiki/Islay" title="Islay">Islay</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cen%C3%A9l_nGabr%C3%A1in" class="mw-redirect" title="Cen\xc3\xa9l nGabr\xc3\xa1in">Cen\xc3\xa9l nGabr\xc3\xa1in</a> (kindred of Gabr\xc3\xa1n) based in <a href="/wiki/Kintyre" title="Kintyre">Kintyre</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cen%C3%A9l_Comgaill" class="mw-redirect" title="Cen\xc3\xa9l Comgaill">Cen\xc3\xa9l Comgaill</a> (kindred of Comgall) based in east Argyll, who gave their name to the district of <a href="/wiki/Cowal" title="Cowal">Cowal</a><sup id="cite_ref-OxfordCompanion1_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordCompanion1-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> sources often referred to the inhabitants of D\xc3\xa1l Riata as <i>Scots</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Scoti" title="Scoti">Scoti</a></i>), a name originally used by Roman and Greek writers for the Irish who raided <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>. Later, it came to refer to <a href="/wiki/Middle_Gaelic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Gaelic language">Gaelic</a>-speakers, whether from Ireland or elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> They are referred to herein as <i><a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaels</a></i>, an unambiguous term, or as <i>D\xc3\xa1l Riatans</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Hillfort" title="Hillfort">hillfort</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dunadd" title="Dunadd">Dunadd</a> is believed to have been its capital. Other royal forts included <a href="/wiki/Dunollie_Castle" title="Dunollie Castle">Dunollie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dunaverty" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunaverty">Dunaverty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dunseverick" title="Dunseverick">Dunseverick</a>. Within D\xc3\xa1l Riata was the important monastery of <a href="/wiki/Iona_Abbey" title="Iona Abbey">Iona</a>, which played a key role in the spread of <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christianity</a> throughout northern Britain, and in the development of <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">insular art</a>. Iona was a centre of learning and produced many important manuscripts. D\xc3\xa1l Riata had a strong <a href="/wiki/Seafaring" class="mw-redirect" title="Seafaring">seafaring</a> culture and a large naval fleet.\n</p><p>D\xc3\xa1l Riata is said to have been founded by the legendary king <a href="/wiki/Fergus_M%C3%B3r" title="Fergus M\xc3\xb3r">Fergus M\xc3\xb3r</a> (Fergus the Great) in the 5th century. The kingdom reached its height under <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed%C3%A1n_mac_Gabr%C3%A1in" title="\xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n mac Gabr\xc3\xa1in">\xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n mac Gabr\xc3\xa1in</a> (<a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Reigned&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template:Reigned (page does not exist)">Template:Reigned</a>). During his reign D\xc3\xa1l Riata\'s power and influence grew; it carried out naval expeditions to <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>, and assaults on the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Brittonic</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Strathclyde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglian</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernicia</a>. However, King <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelfrith_of_Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x86thelfrith of Northumbria">\xc3\x86thelfrith</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernicia</a> checked its growth at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Degsastan" title="Battle of Degsastan">Battle of Degsastan</a> in 603. Serious defeats in Ireland and Scotland during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Domnall_Brecc" title="Domnall Brecc">Domnall Brecc</a> (died 642) ended D\xc3\xa1l Riata\'s "golden age", and the kingdom became a client of <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a> for a time. In the 730s the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Pictish</a> king <a href="/wiki/%C3%93engus_I" title="\xc3\x93engus I">\xc3\x93engus I</a> led campaigns against D\xc3\xa1l Riata and brought it under Pictish overlordship by 741. There is disagreement over the fate of the kingdom from the late 8th century onwards. Some scholars have seen no revival of D\xc3\xa1l Riatan power after the long period of foreign domination (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;637</span> to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;750\xe2\x80\x93760</span>), while others have seen a revival under <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed_Find" title="\xc3\x81ed Find">\xc3\x81ed Find</a> (736\xe2\x80\x93778). Some even claim that the D\xc3\xa1l Riata usurped the kingship of <a href="/wiki/Fortriu" title="Fortriu">Fortriu</a>. From 795 onward there were sporadic <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> raids in D\xc3\xa1l Riata. In the following century, there may have been a merger of the D\xc3\xa1l Riatan and Pictish crowns. Some sources say <a href="/wiki/Cin%C3%A1ed_mac_Ailp%C3%ADn" class="mw-redirect" title="Cin\xc3\xa1ed mac Ailp\xc3\xadn">Cin\xc3\xa1ed mac Ailp\xc3\xadn</a> (Kenneth MacAlpin) was king of D\xc3\xa1l Riata before becoming king of the Picts in 843, following a disastrous defeat of the Picts by Vikings.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The kingdom\'s independence ended sometime after, as it merged with Pictland to form the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">Kingdom of Alba</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=D%C3%A1l_Riata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The name <i><i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</i></i> is derived from <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a>. <i><i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">D\xc3\xa1l</i></i>, cognate to English <i>dole</i> and <i>deal</i>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <i><i lang="de" title="German language text">Teil</i></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">t\xc4\x81li\xc5\x8d</i></i> and descendants including <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <i><i lang="fr" title="French language text">taille</i></i> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> <i><i lang="it" title="Italian language text">taglia</i></i>, means "portion" or "share" (as in "a portion of land"); <i>Riata</i> or <i>Riada</i> is believed to be a personal name.<sup id="cite_ref-bede1_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bede1-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Thus, the name refers to "Riada\'s portion" of territory in the area.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Dalradian" title="Dalradian">Dalradian</a> geological series, a term coined by <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Geikie" title="Archibald Geikie">Archibald Geikie</a> in 1891, was named after D\xc3\xa1l Riata because its outcrop has a similar geographical reach to that of the former kingdom.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="People.2C_land_and_sea"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="People,_land_and_sea">People, land and sea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=D%C3%A1l_Riata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: People, land and sea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="floatright"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulster_%26_the_Hebrides.png" class="image" title="Ulster and the Hebrides"><img alt="Ulster and the Hebrides" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ulster_%26_the_Hebrides.png/200px-Ulster_%26_the_Hebrides.png" width="200" height="263" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ulster_%26_the_Hebrides.png/300px-Ulster_%26_the_Hebrides.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ulster_%26_the_Hebrides.png/400px-Ulster_%26_the_Hebrides.png 2x" data-file-width="418" data-file-height="550" /></a></div>\n<p>D\xc3\xa1l Riata spanned the North Channel and included parts of western Scotland and northeastern Ireland. In Scotland, it corresponded roughly to <a href="/wiki/Argyll" title="Argyll">Argyll</a> (from <i>Airer Go\xc3\xaddel</i>, "coast of the Gaels") and later grew to include <a href="/wiki/Skye" title="Skye">Skye</a>. In Ireland, it took in the northeast of <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">County Antrim</a>, roughly corresponding to the <a href="/wiki/Baronies_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Baronies of Ireland">baronies</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cary_(barony)" title="Cary (barony)">Cary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glenarm_Lower" title="Glenarm Lower">Glenarm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The modern human landscape of D\xc3\xa1l Riata differs a great deal from that of the first millennium. Most people today live in settlements far larger than anything known in early times, while some areas, such as <a href="/wiki/Kilmartin_Glen" title="Kilmartin Glen">Kilmartin</a>, and many of the islands, such as <a href="/wiki/Islay" title="Islay">Islay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiree" title="Tiree">Tiree</a>, may well have had as many inhabitants as they do today. Many of the small settlements have now disappeared, so that the countryside is far emptier than was formerly the case, and many areas that were formerly farmed are now abandoned. Even the physical landscape is not entirely as it was: sea-levels have changed, and the combination of erosion and silting will have considerably altered the shape of the coast in some places, while the natural accumulation of peat and man-made changes from peat-cutting have altered inland landscapes.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As was normal at the time, subsistence farming was the occupation of most people. <a href="/wiki/Oat" title="Oat">Oats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> were the main cereal crops. <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">Pastoralism</a> was especially important, and <a href="/wiki/Transhumance" title="Transhumance">transhumance</a> (the seasonal movement of people with their livestock between fixed summer and winter pastures) was the practice in many places. Some areas, most notably Islay, were especially fertile, and good grazing would have been available all year round, just as it was in Ireland. Tiree was famed in later times for its oats and barley, while smaller, uninhabited islands were used to keep sheep. The area, until lately, was notable for its inshore fisheries, and for plentiful shellfish, therefore seafood is likely to have been an important part of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Senchus_fer_n-Alban" title="Senchus fer n-Alban">Senchus fer n-Alban</a></i> lists three main kin groups in D\xc3\xa1l Riata in Scotland, with a fourth being added later:<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Cen%C3%A9l_nGabr%C3%A1in" class="mw-redirect" title="Cen\xc3\xa9l nGabr\xc3\xa1in">Cen\xc3\xa9l nGabr\xc3\xa1in</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Kintyre" title="Kintyre">Kintyre</a>, supposedly the descendants of <a href="/wiki/Gabr%C3%A1n_mac_Domangairt" title="Gabr\xc3\xa1n mac Domangairt">Gabr\xc3\xa1n mac Domangairt</a>.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Cen%C3%A9l_n%C3%93engusa" title="Cen\xc3\xa9l n\xc3\x93engusa">Cen\xc3\xa9l n\xc3\x93engusa</a>, in Islay and <a href="/wiki/Jura,_Scotland" title="Jura, Scotland">Jura</a>, supposedly the descendants of \xc3\x93engus M\xc3\xb3r mac Eirc.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Loarn_mac_Eirc" title="Loarn mac Eirc">Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Lorne,_Argyll_and_Bute" class="mw-redirect" title="Lorne, Argyll and Bute">Lorne</a>, perhaps also <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Mull" class="mw-redirect" title="Isle of Mull">Mull</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ardnamurchan" title="Ardnamurchan">Ardnamurchan</a>, supposedly the descendants of <a href="/wiki/Loarn_mac_Eirc" title="Loarn mac Eirc">Loarn mac Eirc</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Cen%C3%A9l_Comgaill" class="mw-redirect" title="Cen\xc3\xa9l Comgaill">Cen\xc3\xa9l Comgaill</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Cowal" title="Cowal">Cowal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Bute" title="Isle of Bute">Bute</a>, a later addition, supposedly the descendants of <a href="/wiki/Comgall_mac_Domangairt" title="Comgall mac Domangairt">Comgall mac Domangairt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>The Senchus does not list any kindreds in Ireland, but does list an apparently very minor kindred called Cen\xc3\xa9l Chonchride in Islay descended from another son of Erc, Fergus Becc. Another kindred, Cen\xc3\xa9l B\xc3\xa1et\xc3\xa1in of <a href="/wiki/Morvern" title="Morvern">Morvern</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Clan_MacInnes" title="Clan MacInnes">Clan MacInnes</a>), branched off from Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn about the same time that Cen\xc3\xa9l Comgaill separated from its parent kindred. The <a href="/wiki/Morvern" title="Morvern">Morvern</a> district was formerly known as Kinelvadon, after the Cen\xc3\xa9l B\xc3\xa1et\xc3\xa1in.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn may have been the largest of the "three kindreds", as the Senchus reports it being divided further into Cen\xc3\xa9l Shalaig, Cen\xc3\xa9l Cathbath, Cen\xc3\xa9l nEchdach, Cen\xc3\xa9l Murerdaig. Among the Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn it also lists the <a href="/wiki/Airg%C3%ADalla" title="Airg\xc3\xadalla">Airg\xc3\xadalla</a>, although whether this should be understood as being Irish settlers or simply another tribe to whom the label was applied is unclear. Bannerman proposes a tie to the <a href="/w/index.php?title=U%C3%AD_Macc_Uais&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="U\xc3\xad Macc Uais (page does not exist)">U\xc3\xad Macc Uais</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The meaning of Airg\xc3\xadalla \'hostage givers\' adds to the uncertainty, although it must be observed that only one grouping in Ireland was apparently given this name and it is therefore very rare, perhaps supporting the Ui Macc Uais hypothesis. There is no reason to suppose that this is a complete or accurate list.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Four sites in D\xc3\xa1l Riata may have had royal associations:  <a href="/wiki/Dunadd" title="Dunadd">Dunadd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dunollie_Castle" title="Dunollie Castle">Dunollie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dunaverty" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunaverty">Dunaverty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tarbert" title="Tarbert">Tarbert</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Among them, <a href="/wiki/Dunadd" title="Dunadd">Dunadd</a> appears to have been the most important. It has been partly excavated, and weapons, <a href="/wiki/Quern-stone" title="Quern-stone">quern-stones</a> and many moulds for the manufacture of jewellery were found in addition to fortifications. Other high-status material included glassware and wine <a href="/wiki/Amphora" title="Amphora">amphorae</a> from <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>, and in larger quantities than found elsewhere in Britain and Ireland. Lesser centres included <a href="/wiki/Dunollie_Castle" title="Dunollie Castle">Dun Ollaigh</a>, seat of the Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn kings, and <a href="/wiki/Dunaverty" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunaverty">Dunaverty</a>, at the southern end of Kintyre, in the lands of the Cen\xc3\xa9l nGabr\xc3\xa1in.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The main royal centre in Ireland appears to have been at <a href="/wiki/Dunseverick" title="Dunseverick">Dunseverick</a> (<i>D\xc3\xban Sebuirge</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>D\xc3\xa1l Riata had a strong <a href="/wiki/Seafaring" class="mw-redirect" title="Seafaring">seafaring</a> culture.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> It was an <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> with many islands and <a href="/wiki/Peninsula" title="Peninsula">peninsulas</a>. This, and the difficulty of overland travel, meant that travel by sea was the easiest means of moving any distance. As well as long distance trade, local trade must also have been significant.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Currach" title="Currach">Currachs</a> were probably the most common seagoing craft, and on inland waters <a href="/wiki/Dugout_(boat)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dugout (boat)">dugouts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coracle" title="Coracle">coracles</a> were used. Large timber ships, called "long ships", perhaps similar to the <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> <a href="/wiki/Longship" title="Longship">ships of the same name</a>, are attested to in a variety of sources.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> D\xc3\xa1l Riata had a large <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">war fleet</a> manned by skilled sailors, capable of undertaking far-reaching expeditions. It had an organized system for manning the fleet. Houses were grouped into twenties for the purpose of naval recruitment, with each group having to provide a quota of 28 <a href="/wiki/Rowing" title="Rowing">oarsmen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-duffy586_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-duffy586-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_art">Religion and art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=D%C3%A1l_Riata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Religion and art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Martins_Cross_on_Iona.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/St_Martins_Cross_on_Iona.jpg/200px-St_Martins_Cross_on_Iona.jpg" width="200" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/St_Martins_Cross_on_Iona.jpg/300px-St_Martins_Cross_on_Iona.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/St_Martins_Cross_on_Iona.jpg/400px-St_Martins_Cross_on_Iona.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1789" data-file-height="2396" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St_Martins_Cross_on_Iona.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>9th century St Martin\'s Cross on <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KellsFol028vPortMatt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/KellsFol028vPortMatt.jpg/200px-KellsFol028vPortMatt.jpg" width="200" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/KellsFol028vPortMatt.jpg/300px-KellsFol028vPortMatt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/KellsFol028vPortMatt.jpg/400px-KellsFol028vPortMatt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="1006" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KellsFol028vPortMatt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Saint Matthew, folio 28v in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Kells" title="Book of Kells">Book of Kells</a></div></div></div>\n<p>No written accounts exist for pre-Christian D\xc3\xa1l Riata, and the earliest known records come from the chroniclers of <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a> and Irish monasteries. <a href="/wiki/Adomn%C3%A1n" title="Adomn\xc3\xa1n">Adomn\xc3\xa1n</a>\'s <i>Life of St <a href="/wiki/Columba" title="Columba">Columba</a></i> implies a Christian D\xc3\xa1l Riata.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Whether this is true cannot be known. The figure of Columba looms large in any history of Christianity in D\xc3\xa1l Riata. Adomn\xc3\xa1n\'s <i>Life</i>, although useful as a record, was not intended to serve as history, but rather as <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiography</a>. Because the writing of the lives of the saints in Adomn\xc3\xa1n\'s day had not reached the stylised formulas of the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, the <i>Life</i> contains a great deal of historically valuable information. It is also a vital linguistic source indicating the distribution of Gaelic and P-Celtic placenames in northern Scotland by the end of the 7th century. It famously notes Columba\'s need for a translator when conversing with an individual on Skye.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> This evidence of a non-Gaelic language is supported by a sprinkling of P-Celtic placenames on the remote mainland opposite the island.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Columba\'s founding Iona within the bounds of D\xc3\xa1l Riata ensured that the kingdom would be of great importance in the spread of Christianity in northern Britain, not only to Pictland, but also to Northumbria, via <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">Lindisfarne</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a>, and beyond. Although the monastery of Iona belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Cen%C3%A9l_Conaill" class="mw-redirect" title="Cen\xc3\xa9l Conaill">Cen\xc3\xa9l Conaill</a> of the Northern U\xc3\xad N\xc3\xa9ill, and not to D\xc3\xa1l Riata, it had close ties to the Cen\xc3\xa9l nGabr\xc3\xa1in, ties which may make the annals less than entirely impartial.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>If Iona was the greatest religious centre in D\xc3\xa1l Riata, it was far from unique. <a href="/wiki/Lismore,_Scotland" title="Lismore, Scotland">Lismore</a>, in the territory of the Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn, was sufficiently important for the death of its abbots to be recorded with some frequency. <a href="/wiki/Applecross" title="Applecross">Applecross</a>, probably in Pictish territory for most of the period, and <a href="/wiki/Kingarth" title="Kingarth">Kingarth</a> on Bute are also known to have been monastic sites, and many smaller sites, such as on <a href="/wiki/Eigg" title="Eigg">Eigg</a> and Tiree, are known from the annals.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In Ireland, <a href="/wiki/Armoy" class="mw-redirect" title="Armoy">Armoy</a> was the main ecclesiastical centre in early times, associated with <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick" title="Saint Patrick">Saint Patrick</a> and with <a href="/wiki/Saint_Olc%C3%A1n" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Olc\xc3\xa1n">Saint Olc\xc3\xa1n</a>, said to have been first bishop at Armoy. An important early centre, Armoy later declined, overshadowed by the monasteries at Movilla (<a href="/wiki/Newtownards" title="Newtownards">Newtownards</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Bangor,_County_Down" title="Bangor, County Down">Bangor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As well as their primary spiritual importance, the political significance of religious centres cannot be dismissed. The prestige of being associated with the saintly founder was of no small importance. Monasteries represented a source of wealth as well as prestige. Additionally, the learning and literacy found in monasteries served as useful tools for ambitious kings.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscript</a> <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Kells" title="Book of Kells">Book of Kells</a> was probably at least begun at Iona, although not by Columba as legend has it, as it dates from about 800 (it may have been commissioned to mark the bicentennial of Columba\'s death in 597). Whether it was or not, Iona was certainly important in the formation of <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">Insular art</a>, which combined Mediterranean, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_art" title="Anglo-Saxon art">Anglo-Saxon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celtic_art" title="Celtic art">Celtic</a> and Pictish elements into a style of which the book of Kells is a late example.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>For other arts, a number of sculptures remain to give an impression of D\xc3\xa1l Riatan work. The St. Martin\'s Cross on Iona is the best-preserved <a href="/wiki/High_cross" title="High cross">high cross</a>, probably inspired by Northumbrian free-standing crosses, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ruthwell_Cross" title="Ruthwell Cross">Ruthwell Cross</a>, although a similar cross exists in Ireland (<a href="/wiki/Ahenny" title="Ahenny">Ahenny</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Tipperary" title="County Tipperary">County Tipperary</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Kildalton_Cross" title="Kildalton Cross">Kildalton Cross</a> on Islay is similar. A sculpted slab at <a href="/wiki/Ardchattan" class="mw-redirect" title="Ardchattan">Ardchattan</a> appears to show strong Pictish influences, while the <a href="/wiki/Dupplin_Cross" title="Dupplin Cross">Dupplin Cross</a>, it has been argued, shows that influences also moved in the opposite direction. Fine <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Hiberno-Saxon">Hiberno-Saxon</a> metalwork such as <a href="/wiki/Penannular_brooches" class="mw-redirect" title="Penannular brooches">penannular brooches</a> is believed to have been created at Dunadd.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to the monastic sites, a considerable number of churches are attested, not only from archaeological evidence, but also from the evidence of place-names. The element "kil", from Gaelic <i>cill</i>, can be shown in many cases to be associated with early churches, such as at Kilmartin by Dunadd.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=D%C3%A1l_Riata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=D%C3%A1l_Riata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg/220px-Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg/330px-Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg/440px-Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1248" data-file-height="1008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_Dunadd_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Footprint (replica<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>) used in king-making ceremonies, Dunadd</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Duan_Albanach" title="Duan Albanach">Duan Albanach</a></i> (Song of the Scots) tells that the three sons of <a href="/wiki/Erc_of_Dalriada" title="Erc of Dalriada">Erc</a>\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Fergus_M%C3%B3r" title="Fergus M\xc3\xb3r">Fergus M\xc3\xb3r</a>, <a href="/wiki/Loarn_mac_Eirc" title="Loarn mac Eirc">Loarn</a> and \xc3\x93engus\xe2\x80\x94conquered <i>Alba</i> (Scotland) in around 500. <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> offers a different, and probably older, account wherein D\xc3\xa1l Riata was conquered by Irish Gaels led by a certain <i>Reuda</i>. Old Gaelic <i>D\xc3\xa1l</i> means "portion" or "share", and is usually followed by the name of an <a href="/wiki/Eponym" title="Eponym">eponymous</a> founder.<sup id="cite_ref-bede1_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bede1-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Bede\'s tale may come from the same root as the Irish tales of Cairpre Riata and his brothers, the <a href="/wiki/S%C3%ADl_Conairi" title="S\xc3\xadl Conairi">S\xc3\xadl Conairi</a> (sons/descendants of <a href="/wiki/Conaire_M%C3%B3r" title="Conaire M\xc3\xb3r">Conaire M\xc3\xb3r</a> / <a href="/wiki/Conaire_C%C3%B3em" title="Conaire C\xc3\xb3em">Conaire C\xc3\xb3em</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The story of D\xc3\xa1l Riata moves from <a href="/wiki/Myth_of_origins" class="mw-redirect" title="Myth of origins">foundation myth</a> to something nearer to history with the reports of the death of <a href="/wiki/Comgall_mac_Domangairt" title="Comgall mac Domangairt">Comgall mac Domangairt</a> around 540 and of his brother <a href="/wiki/Gabr%C3%A1n_mac_Domangairt" title="Gabr\xc3\xa1n mac Domangairt">Gabr\xc3\xa1n</a> around 560.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The version of history in the <i>Duan Albanach</i> was long accepted, although it is preceded by the purely fictional tale of <a href="/wiki/Albanactus" title="Albanactus">Albanus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brutus_of_Troy" title="Brutus of Troy">Brutus</a> conquering Britain. The presence of Gaelic in Scotland was seen as the result of either a large-scale migration from Ireland,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> or a takeover by Irish Gaelic elites. However, this theory is no longer universally accepted. In his academic paper <i>Were the Scots Irish?</i>, archaeologist Dr Ewan Campbell says that there is no archaeological or placename evidence of a migration or takeover.<sup id="cite_ref-ewancampbell_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewancampbell-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> This lack of archaeological evidence was previously noted by Professor <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Alcock" title="Leslie Alcock">Leslie Alcock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ewancampbell_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewancampbell-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Archaeological evidence shows that Argyll was different from Ireland, before and after the supposed migration, but that it also formed part of the Irish Sea province with Ireland, being easily distinguished from the rest of Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Campbell suggests that Argyll and Antrim formed a "maritime province", united by the sea and isolated from the rest of Scotland by the mountainous ridge called the <i>Druim Alban</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ewancampbell_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewancampbell-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> This allowed a shared language to be maintained through the centuries; Argyll remained Gaelic-speaking while the rest of Scotland was either <a href="/wiki/Pictish_language" title="Pictish language">Pictish</a> or <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic</a>-speaking.<sup id="cite_ref-ewancampbell_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewancampbell-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Campbell argues that the medieval accounts were a kind of dynastic propaganda, constructed to bolster a dynasty\'s claim to the throne and to bolster D\xc3\xa1l Riata claims to territory in Antrim.<sup id="cite_ref-ewancampbell_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewancampbell-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> This view of the medieval accounts is shared by other historians.<sup id="cite_ref-ewancampbell_36-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ewancampbell-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However D\xc3\xa1l Riata came to be, the time in which it arose was one of great instability in Ulster, following the <a href="/wiki/Ulaid" title="Ulaid">Ulaid</a>\'s loss of territory (including the ancient centre of Emain Macha) to the <a href="/wiki/Airg%C3%ADalla" title="Airg\xc3\xadalla">Airg\xc3\xadalla</a> and the <a href="/wiki/U%C3%AD_N%C3%A9ill" title="U\xc3\xad N\xc3\xa9ill">U\xc3\xad N\xc3\xa9ill</a>. Whether the two parts of D\xc3\xa1l Riata had long been united, or whether a conquest in the 4th century or early 5th century, either of Antrim from Argyll, or vice versa, in line with myth, is not known. "The thriving of Dalriada", pp.&#160;47\xe2\x80\x9350, notes that a conquest of Irish D\xc3\xa1l Riata from Scotland, in the period after the fall of Emain Macha, fits the facts as well as any other hypothesis.\n</p><p>Linguistic and genealogical evidence associates ancestors of the D\xc3\xa1l Riata with the prehistoric <a href="/wiki/Iverni" title="Iverni">Iverni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Darini" title="Darini">Darini</a>, suggesting kinship with the Ulaid and a number of shadowy kingdoms in distant <a href="/wiki/Munster" title="Munster">Munster</a>. The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Robogdii&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Robogdii (page does not exist)">Robogdii</a> have also been suggested as ancestral.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately, the D\xc3\xa1l Riata, according to the earliest genealogies, are descendants of <a href="/wiki/Deda_mac_Sin" title="Deda mac Sin">Deda mac Sin</a>, a prehistoric king or deity of the \xc3\x89rainn.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Druimm_Cete_to_Mag_Rath">Druimm Cete to Mag Rath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=D%C3%A1l_Riata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Druimm Cete to Mag Rath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dalriada.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Dalriada.jpg/200px-Dalriada.jpg" width="200" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Dalriada.jpg/300px-Dalriada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Dalriada.jpg/400px-Dalriada.jpg 2x" data-file-width="509" data-file-height="531" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dalriada.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of D\xc3\xa1l Riata at its height, c. 580\xe2\x80\x93600. <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Pictish</a> regions are marked in yellow.</div></div></div>\n<p>By the mid 6th-century, the D\xc3\xa1l Riata in Scotland came under serious threat from <a href="/wiki/Bridei_I" title="Bridei I">Bridei I</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="King of the Picts">king of the Picts</a>, whilst the Irish portion faced hostility from the <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_nAraidi" title="D\xc3\xa1l nAraidi">D\xc3\xa1l nAraidi</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ulaid" title="Ulaid">Ulaid</a>, resulting in them seeking the aid of the Irish <a href="/wiki/Northern_U%C3%AD_N%C3%A9ill" title="Northern U\xc3\xad N\xc3\xa9ill">Northern U\xc3\xad N\xc3\xa9ill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bardon17_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bardon17-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fraser2007pg316-9_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fraser2007pg316-9-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> D\xc3\xa1l Riata reached its greatest extent in the reign of \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n mac Gabr\xc3\xa1in, who was said to have been consecrated by Columba,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> who \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n granted the island of <a href="/wiki/Iona" title="Iona">Iona</a> off the coast of Scotland. Columba, who was also a <a href="/wiki/Cen%C3%A9l_Conaill" class="mw-redirect" title="Cen\xc3\xa9l Conaill">Cen\xc3\xa9l Conaill</a> prince, negotiated an alliance between D\xc3\xa1l Riata and the Cen\xc3\xa9l Conaill, who were the ruling dynasty of the Northern U\xc3\xad N\xc3\xa9ill, in 575 at the Convention of Druimm Cete near <a href="/wiki/Limavady" title="Limavady">Limavady</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bardon17_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bardon17-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McSparron109_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McSparron109-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In attendance were Columba, \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n mac Gabr\xc3\xa1in, and <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed_mac_Ainmuirech" title="\xc3\x81ed mac Ainmuirech">\xc3\x81ed mac Ainmuirech</a>, king of the Northern U\xc3\xad N\xc3\xa9ill and <a href="/wiki/High_King_of_Ireland" title="High King of Ireland">High King of Ireland</a>.\n</p><p>What was actually discussed at Druimm Cete is the matter of debate with various suggestions provided that it was: to determine the constitutional status of both parts of D\xc3\xa1l Riata; to determine the status of Irish D\xc3\xa1l Riata only with it having its own king; that D\xc3\xa1l Riata was to become independent of the High King of Ireland;<sup id="cite_ref-Fraser2007pg316-9_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fraser2007pg316-9-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> that the Ireland part of D\xc3\xa1l Riata would pay tribute to the High King and support him with land forces, and that the Scotland part would be independent but support the High King with its fleet when needed;<sup id="cite_ref-duffy586_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-duffy586-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> the removal of D\xc3\xa1l Riata from Ulaid\'s overlordship, allowing it to concentrate on extending its Scottish domain.<sup id="cite_ref-Bardon17_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bardon17-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> What is certain is that both parties had the D\xc3\xa1l nAraidi as a common foe.<sup id="cite_ref-Fraser2007pg316-9_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fraser2007pg316-9-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This pact between the D\xc3\xa1l Riata and Cen\xc3\xa9l Conaill was successful, first in defeating B\xc3\xa1etan mac Cairill, king of the D\xc3\xa1l nAraidi, then in allowing \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n to campaign widely against his neighbours, as far afield as <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> and lands of the <a href="/wiki/Maeatae" title="Maeatae">Maeatae</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/River_Forth" title="River Forth">River Forth</a>. \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n appears to have been very successful in extending his power, until he faced the <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernician</a> king <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelfrith_of_Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x86thelfrith of Northumbria">\xc3\x86thelfrith</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Degsastan" title="Battle of Degsastan">Degsastan</a> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;603</span>. \xc3\x86thelfrith\'s brother was among the dead, but \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n was defeated, and the Bernician kings continued their advances in southern Scotland. \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n died <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;608</span> aged about 70. D\xc3\xa1l Riata did expand to include <a href="/wiki/Skye" title="Skye">Skye</a>, possibly conquered by \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n\'s son Gartnait.\n</p><p>It has been suggested that <a href="/wiki/Fiachnae_mac_B%C3%A1et%C3%A1in" title="Fiachnae mac B\xc3\xa1et\xc3\xa1in">Fiachnae mac B\xc3\xa1et\xc3\xa1in</a> (died 626), D\xc3\xa1l nAraidi over-king of Ulaid, was overlord of both parts of D\xc3\xa1l Riata. Fiachnae campaigned against the Northumbrians, and besieged <a href="/wiki/Bamburgh" title="Bamburgh">Bamburgh</a>, and the D\xc3\xa1l Riatans are thought to have fought in this campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 629 the D\xc3\xa1l Riata suffered significant loses at the battle of Fid Euin where the D\xc3\xa1l nAraidi, led by <a href="/wiki/Congal_C%C3%A1ech" title="Congal C\xc3\xa1ech">Congal C\xc3\xa1ech mac Scandl\xc3\xa1in</a>, killed the D\xc3\xa1l Riata king as well as three grandsons of \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n mac Gabr\xc3\xa1in. It is suggested to have been an achievement that D\xc3\xa1l Riata itself survived this battle.<sup id="cite_ref-Fraser2007pg316-9_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fraser2007pg316-9-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> That same year the Cen\xc3\xa9l Conaill defeated Congal C\xc3\xa1ech at the battle of D\xc3\xban Ceithirn.<sup id="cite_ref-Fraser2007pg316-9_40-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fraser2007pg316-9-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>D\xc3\xa1l Riata remained allied with the Northern U\xc3\xad N\xc3\xa9ill until the reign of Domnall Brecc, who was persuaded by the king of D\xc3\xa1l nAraidi, <a href="/wiki/Congal_C%C3%A1ech" title="Congal C\xc3\xa1ech">Congal C\xc3\xa1ech</a>, to renounce this alliance.<sup id="cite_ref-McSparron109_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McSparron109-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In an attempt to have himself installed as <a href="/wiki/High_King_of_Ireland" title="High King of Ireland">High King of Ireland</a>, Congal made alliances with D\xc3\xa1l Riata and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Strathclyde</a>, which resulted in the disastrous <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Magh_Rath" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Magh Rath">Battle of Magh Rath</a> in 637, which saw Congal slain by High King <a href="/wiki/Domnall_mac_%C3%81edo" title="Domnall mac \xc3\x81edo">Domnall mac \xc3\x81edo</a> of the Northern U\xc3\xad N\xc3\xa9ill and resulted in Irish D\xc3\xa1l Riata losing possession of its Scottish lands.<sup id="cite_ref-Bardon20-1_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bardon20-1-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> A battle had also taken place at sea at Sailt\xc3\xadr, off Kintyre, in 637. This defeat was then attributed as divine retribution for Domnall Brecc turning his back on his prior alliance.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Domnall Brecc\'s policy appears to have died with him in 642, at his final, and fatal, defeat by <a href="/wiki/Eugein_I_of_Alt_Clut" title="Eugein I of Alt Clut">Eugein map Beli</a> of Strathclyde at <a href="/wiki/River_Carron_(Forth)" class="mw-redirect" title="River Carron (Forth)">Strathcarron</a>, for as late as the 730s, armies and fleets from D\xc3\xa1l Riata fought alongside the U\xc3\xad N\xc3\xa9ill.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This defeat shattered the power of D\xc3\xa1l Riata as well as that of D\xc3\xa1l nAraidi, allowing the Northern U\xc3\xad N\xc3\xa9ill to become the dominant force in the north of Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-McSparron109_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McSparron109-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> By the 10th-century, the Irish lands of D\xc3\xa1l Riata were under the control of the <a href="/wiki/U%C3%AD_Tuirtri" class="mw-redirect" title="U\xc3\xad Tuirtri">U\xc3\xad Tuirtri</a>, and their clients, the Fir L\xc3\xad.<sup id="cite_ref-McSparron109_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McSparron109-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mag_Rath_to_the_Pictish_Conquest">Mag Rath to the Pictish Conquest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=D%C3%A1l_Riata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mag Rath to the Pictish Conquest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>It has been proposed that some of the more obscure kings of D\xc3\xa1l Riata mentioned in the Annals of Ulster, such as <a href="/wiki/Fiannamail_ua_D%C3%BAnchado" title="Fiannamail ua D\xc3\xbanchado">Fiannamail ua D\xc3\xbanchado</a> and <a href="/wiki/Donncoirce" title="Donncoirce">Donncoirce</a>, may have been kings of Irish D\xc3\xa1l Riata.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The after-effect of the Battle of Moira in regards to Scottish D\xc3\xa1l Riata appears to have resulted in it becoming tributary to Northumbrian kings, which lasted until the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Pictish</a> king <a href="/wiki/Bridei_III_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Bridei III of the Picts">Bruide mac Bili</a> defeated <a href="/wiki/Ecgfrith_of_Northumbria" title="Ecgfrith of Northumbria">Ecgfrith of Northumbria</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dun_Nechtain" title="Battle of Dun Nechtain">Dun Nechtain</a> in 685. It is not certain that this subjection ended in 685, although this is usually assumed to be the case.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> However, it appears that <a href="/wiki/Eadberht_of_Northumbria" title="Eadberht of Northumbria">Eadberht Eating</a> made some effort to stop the Picts under <a href="/wiki/%C3%93engus_I_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x93engus I of the Picts">\xc3\x93engus mac Fergusa</a> crushing D\xc3\xa1l Riata in 740. Whether this means that the tributary relationship had not ended in 685, or if Eadberht sought only to prevent the growth of Pictish power, is unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since it has been thought that D\xc3\xa1l Riata swallowed Pictland to create the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">Kingdom of Alba</a>, the later history of D\xc3\xa1l Riata has tended to be seen as a prelude to future triumphs.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The annals make it clear that the Cen\xc3\xa9l Gabra\xc3\xadn lost any earlier monopoly of royal power in the late 7th century and in the 8th, when Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn kings such as <a href="/wiki/Ferchar_Fota" title="Ferchar Fota">Ferchar Fota</a>, his son <a href="/wiki/Selbach_mac_Ferchair" title="Selbach mac Ferchair">Selbach</a>, and grandsons <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BAngal_mac_Selbaig" title="D\xc3\xbangal mac Selbaig">D\xc3\xbangal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muiredach_mac_Ainbcellaig" title="Muiredach mac Ainbcellaig">Muiredach</a> are found contesting for the kingship of D\xc3\xa1l Riata. The long period of instability in D\xc3\xa1l Riata was only ended by the conquest of the kingdom by \xc3\x93engus mac Fergusa, king of the Picts, in the 730s. After a third campaign by \xc3\x93engus in 741, D\xc3\xa1l Riata then disappears from the Irish records for a generation.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_last_century">The last century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=D%C3%A1l_Riata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The last century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed_Find" title="\xc3\x81ed Find">\xc3\x81ed Find</a> may appear in 768, fighting against the Pictish king of <a href="/wiki/Fortriu" title="Fortriu">Fortriu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> At his death in 778 \xc3\x81ed Find is called "king of D\xc3\xa1l Riata", as is his brother <a href="/wiki/Fergus_mac_Echdach" title="Fergus mac Echdach">Fergus mac Echdach</a> in 781.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The Annals of Ulster say that a certain Donncoirche, "king of D\xc3\xa1l Riata" died in 792, and there the record ends. Any number of theories have been advanced to fill the missing generations, none of which are founded on any very solid evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> A number of kings are named in the <i>Duan Albanach</i>, and in royal genealogies, but these are rather less reliable than we might wish. The obvious conclusion is that whoever ruled the petty kingdoms of D\xc3\xa1l Riata after its defeat and conquest in the 730s, only \xc3\x81ed Find and his brother Fergus drew the least attention of the chroniclers in Iona and Ireland. This argues very strongly for Alex Woolf\'s conclusion that \xc3\x93engus mac Fergusa "effectively destroyed the kingdom".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is unlikely that D\xc3\xa1l Riata was ruled directly by Pictish kings, but it is argued that <a href="/wiki/Domnall_mac_Caustant%C3%ADn" title="Domnall mac Caustant\xc3\xadn">Domnall</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Caustant%C3%ADn_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Caustant\xc3\xadn of the Picts">Caustant\xc3\xadn mac Fergusa</a>, was king of D\xc3\xa1l Riata from 811 to 835. He was apparently followed by the last named king of D\xc3\xa1l Riata <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed_mac_Boanta" title="\xc3\x81ed mac Boanta">\xc3\x81ed mac Boanta</a>, who was killed in the great Pictish defeat of 839 at the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 9th century, the Picts were becoming <a href="/wiki/Gaelicized" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaelicized">Gaelicized</a>, and it is suggested that there was a merger of the D\xc3\xa1l Riatan and Pictish kingships.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Traditionally, this is attributed to <a href="/wiki/Cin%C3%A1ed_mac_Ailp%C3%ADn" class="mw-redirect" title="Cin\xc3\xa1ed mac Ailp\xc3\xadn">Cin\xc3\xa1ed mac Ailp\xc3\xadn</a> (Kenneth MacAlpin), who became king of the Picts in about 843. Some sources say that Cin\xc3\xa1ed was king of D\xc3\xa1l Riata for two years before this. Under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Alpin" title="House of Alpin">House of Alpin</a>, D\xc3\xa1l Riata and Pictland merged to form the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">Kingdom of Alba</a> or Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="From_D.C3.A1l_Riata_to_the_Innse_Gall"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="From_D\xc3\xa1l_Riata_to_the_Innse_Gall">From D\xc3\xa1l Riata to the Innse Gall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=D%C3%A1l_Riata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: From D\xc3\xa1l Riata to the Innse Gall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>If the Vikings had a great impact on Pictland and in Ireland, in D\xc3\xa1l Riata, as in Northumbria, they appear to have entirely replaced the existing kingdom with a new entity. In the case of D\xc3\xa1l Riata, this was to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_the_Isles" title="Kingdom of the Isles">kingdom of the Sudreys</a>, traditionally founded by <a href="/wiki/Ketil_Flatnose" class="mw-redirect" title="Ketil Flatnose">Ketil Flatnose</a> (<i>Caitill Find</i> in Gaelic) in the middle of the 9th century. The <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Frankish</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Annales_Bertiniani" title="Annales Bertiniani">Annales Bertiniani</a></i> may record the conquest of the <a href="/wiki/Inner_Hebrides" title="Inner Hebrides">Inner Hebrides</a>, the seaward part of D\xc3\xa1l Riata, by Vikings in 847.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alex_Woolf" title="Alex Woolf">Alex Woolf</a> has suggested that there occurred a formal division of D\xc3\xa1l Riata between the <a href="/wiki/Norse-Gaels" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse-Gaels">Norse-Gaelic</a> <a href="/wiki/U%C3%AD_%C3%8Dmair" title="U\xc3\xad \xc3\x8dmair">U\xc3\xad \xc3\x8dmair</a> and the natives, like those divisions that took place elsewhere in Ireland and Britain, with the Norse controlling most of the islands, and the Gaels controlling the Scottish coast and the more southerly islands. In turn, Woolf suggests that this gave rise to the terms <i>Airer Gaedel</i> and <i>Innse Gall</i>, respectively "the coast of the Gaels" and the "Islands of the foreigners".<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Under_the_House_of_Alpin">Under the House of Alpin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=D%C3%A1l_Riata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Under the House of Alpin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Woolf has further demonstrated that, by the time of <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_II_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm II of Scotland">Malcolm II</a>, the leading <i>cenela</i> of D\xc3\xa1l Riata had moved from the southwest of the region (north of the Firths) to the north, east, and northeast, with Cenel Loairn moving up the <a href="/wiki/Great_Glen" title="Great Glen">Great Glen</a> to occupy <a href="/wiki/Mormaerdom_of_Moray" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaerdom of Moray">Moray</a>, the former and sometimes still <a href="/wiki/Fortriu" title="Fortriu">Fortriu</a>, one branch of Cenel nGabhrain occupying the district known as <a href="/wiki/Gowrie" title="Gowrie">Gowrie</a> and another the district of <a href="/wiki/Mormaerdom_of_Fife" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaerdom of Fife">Fife</a>, Cenel nOengusa giving its name to <a href="/wiki/Circinn" class="mw-redirect" title="Circinn">Circinn</a> as <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Angus" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaer of Angus">Angus</a>, Cenel Comgaill occupying <a href="/wiki/Mormaerdom_of_Strathearn" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaerdom of Strathearn">Strathearn</a>, and another lesser known kindred, Cenel Conaing, probably moving to <a href="/wiki/Mormaerdom_of_Mar" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormaerdom of Mar">Mar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_fiction">In fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=D%C3%A1l_Riata&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: In fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Rosemary_Sutcliff" title="Rosemary Sutcliff">Rosemary Sutcliff</a>\'s 1965 novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mark_of_the_Horse_Lord" title="The Mark of the Horse Lord">The Mark of the Horse Lord</a></i>, the D\xc3\xa1l Riada undergo an internal struggle for control of royal succession, and an external conflict to defend their frontiers against the <a href="/wiki/Caledonians" title="Caledonians">Caledones</a>.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Rosemary_Sutcliff" title="Rosemary Sutcliff">Rosemary Sutcliff</a>\'s historical adventure novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Eagle_of_the_Ninth" title="The Eagle of the Ninth">The Eagle of the Ninth</a></i> (1954), a young Roman officer searches to recover the lost Roman eagle standard of his father\'s legion in the northern part of Great Britain. The story is based on the <a href="/wiki/Legio_IX_Hispana" title="Legio IX Hispana">Ninth Spanish Legion</a>\'s supposed disappearance in the Scottish Highlands near the end of the Roman occupation. The novel was adapted by <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Brock" title="Jeremy Brock">Jeremy Brock</a> into the film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Eagle_(2011_film)" title="The Eagle (2011 film)">The Eagle</a></i> (2011).\n</p><p>In the Kushiel novels (a series, beginning with <i><a href="/wiki/Kushiel%27s_Dart" title="Kushiel&#39;s Dart">Kushiel\'s Dart</a></i>, 2001), by <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Carey" title="Jacqueline Carey">Jacqueline Carey</a>, the Dalriada of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Kingdom of Alba">Kingdom of Alba</a> figure prominently in a Royal marriage and subsequent alliance with France (known in the series as "Terre d\'Ange").\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Julian_May" title="Julian May">Julian May</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Saga_of_Pliocene_Exile" title="Saga of Pliocene Exile">Saga of Pliocene Exile</a> series, the non-born Aiken Drum\'s homeworld is an ethnic Scottish planet called Dalriada.\n</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Lost_Girl" title="Lost Girl">Lost Girl</a></i> TV series, the pub where the Light Fae and the Dark Fae mingle is called the Dal Riata; named after the ancient kingdom.\n</p><p>In Jules Watson\'s Dalriada Trilogy (2006\xe2\x80\x932008), three centuries are chronicled during the time of <a href="/wiki/The_Roman_Invasion_of_Britain" title="The Roman Invasion of Britain">the Roman Invasion of Britain</a>.\n</p><p>A feature length fantasy film previously named <i>Dalriata\'s King</i>, now named <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gaelic_King" title="The Gaelic King">The Gaelic King</a></i> is being made in Scotland, with a story based loosely on the first king of the Scots. It is currently in postproduction and the release date is set for early 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>D\xc3\xa1l Riata is a playable nation in <a href="/wiki/Paradox_Interactive" title="Paradox Interactive">Paradox Interactive</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/4X" title="4X">4X</a> <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">video game</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Crusader_Kings_II" title="Crusader Kings II">Crusader Kings II</a></i>. At the earliest start date, 769 with the <i>Charlemagne</i> <a href="/wiki/Downloadable_content" title="Downloadable content">DLC</a>, they are an <a href="/wiki/Irish_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Catholic">Irish Catholic</a> independent <a href="/wiki/Petty_kingdom" title="Petty kingdom">petty kingdom</a> ruled by <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ed_Find" title="\xc3\x81ed Find">\xc3\x81ed Find</a>, comprising the Hebrides and <a href="/wiki/Argyll" title="Argyll">Argyll</a>. The game will also use D\xc3\xa1l Riata to refer to the Duchy of the Isles whenever the realm\'s top liege is Irish.\n</p><p>Dalriada is the name of a Hungarian folk metal band: <i><a href="/wiki/Dalriada_(band)" title="Dalriada (band)">Dalriada</a></i>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration{color:#555}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription span,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration span{border-bottom:1px .cs1-kern-right,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}</style></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Charles-Edwards, <i>Early Christian Ireland</i>, p. 159\xe2\x80\x93160, considers whether the Latin terms <i>Scotti</i> and <i>Atacotti</i> refer to the confederations in Ulster and <a href="/wiki/Leinster" title="Leinster">Leinster</a> respectively. The etymology of <i>Scotti</i>, and its Gaelic roots, if any, are uncertain. The term in late Classical sources is either specifically linked to raiders from Ireland, or is geographically ambiguous. In sharp contrast, no clear reference pointing to Scotti in Scotland in the Roman period has been found. Despite several references listing different combinations of Picti, Scotti, Hiberni, Attecotti and Saxons together as later Roman Britain\'s archetypal enemies, it is worth noting that \'Scotti\' and \'Hiberni\' are never listed together, confirming that they were then, as they were later, alternative names for the Irish or confederations of the Irish. Regardless of the original sense, or its modern popularity, to use the term "Scot" in this context invites confusion.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <i><a href="/wiki/1066_And_All_That" class="mw-redirect" title="1066 And All That">1066 And All That</a></i>, p. 5, for a parody of the confusion the word "Scot" engenders in this context.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Smyth, and Bannerman, <i>Scottish Takeover</i>, present this case, arguing that Pictish kings from <a href="/wiki/Ciniod_II_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Ciniod II of the Picts">Ciniod son of Uuredech</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caustant%C3%ADn_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Caustant\xc3\xadn of the Picts">Caustant\xc3\xadn</a> onwards were descendants of <a href="/wiki/Fergus_mac_Echdach" title="Fergus mac Echdach">Fergus mac Echdach</a> and Feradach, son of <a href="/wiki/Selbach_mac_Ferchair" title="Selbach mac Ferchair">Selbach mac Ferchair</a>. Broun\'s <i>Pictish Kings</i> offers an alternative reconstruction, and one which has attracted considerable support, e.g. Clancy, "Iona in the kingdom of the Picts: a note", Woolf, <i>Pictland to Alba</i>, pp 57\xe2\x80\x9367.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-bede1-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-bede1_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-bede1_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Bede, HE, Book I, Chapter 1.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Boyd, Hugh Alexander. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://antrimhistory.net/irish-dalriada-by-hugh-alexander-boyd/">Irish Dalriada</a>. <i>The Glynns: Journal of The Glens of Antrim Historical Society</i>. Volume 76 (1978).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See McDonald, <i>Kingdom of the Isles</i>, pp.10\xe2\x80\x9320, for a short discussion of the geography of D\xc3\xa1l Riata in Scotland.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Campbell, <i>Saints and Sea-kings</i>, pp. 22\xe2\x80\x9329; Foster, <i>Picts, Gaels and Scots</i>, pp. 49\xe2\x80\x9359.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Senchus is translated in Bannerman, <i>Studies</i>, pp. 47\xe2\x80\x9349; previously published in <i>Celtica</i>, vols. 7 (1966) \xe2\x80\x93 9 (1971); earlier translations in Anderson, <i>ESSH</i>, vol. 1, pp. cl\xe2\x80\x93cliii and Skene, <i>Chronicles of the Picts and Scots</i>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Broun, ""D\xc3\xa1l Riata", notes that the Senchus treats the Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn differently. In fact, it lists the three (actually four) thirds of the Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn as the Cen\xc3\xa9l Shalaig (or Cen\xc3\xa9l Fergusa Shalaig), Cen\xc3\xa9l Cathbath, Cen\xc3\xa9l nEchdach and Cen\xc3\xa9l Muiredaig. Even the compiler of the Senchus doubts whether their <a href="/wiki/Eponym" title="Eponym">eponymous</a> founders Fergus Shalaig, Cathbad, Eochaid and Muiredach were all sons of Loarn mac Eirc.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bannerman, <i>Studies</i>, p. 110, dates the separation of the Cen\xc3\xa9l Comgaill from the Cen\xc3\xa9l nGabr\xc3\xa1in to around 700.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Watson, <i>Celtic Place-names of Scotland</i>, p. 122.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bannerman, <i>Studies</i>, pp. 115\xe2\x80\x93118. See also Bannerman, <i>Studies</i>, pp. 120 &amp; 122, noting that the <i>Tripartite Life</i> of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick" title="Saint Patrick">Saint Patrick</a> appears to refer to a "Cen\xc3\xa9l n\xc3\x93engusa" in Antrim.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>The Annals of Ulster</i>, s.a. 670, refer to the return of the <i>genus Gartnaith</i>, i.e. the Cen\xc3\xa9l Gartnait, from Ireland to Skye. This Gartnait is presumed to be a son of \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n mac Gabra\xc3\xadn: see Broun, "D\xc3\xa1l Riata". Bannerman, <i>Studies</i>, pp. 92\xe2\x80\x9394, identifies this Gartnait as a son of \xc3\x81ed\xc3\xa1n, whom he sees as the same person as <a href="/wiki/Gartnait_IV_of_the_Picts" class="mw-redirect" title="Gartnait IV of the Picts">Gartnait</a>, king of the Picts. No such son is named by Adomn\xc3\xa1n, in the annals, or by the Senchus. See also Adomn\xc3\xa1n, <i>Life</i>, II, 22, and note 258, where a certain Ioan mac Conaill mac Domnaill is said to have belonged to "the royal lineage of Cen\xc3\xa9l nGabr\xc3\xa1in". See also the discussion of the Cen\xc3\xa9l Loairn above.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">L.
